Barrie's timeless tale Join Wendy, John, and Michael Darling as they follow Peter Pan, the boy who never grows up, to a world where fairies live and children can fly. But beware -- danger abounds in this magical land of mermaids, Indians, and fairy dust. Captain Hook and his pirate crew want all children to walk the plank, especially Peter Pan.<br /><br />There is always an adventure to be had in Neverland. So come along with the Darling children as they soar into the night sky -- second to the right and straight on till morning!</span>

In Peter Pan itself, Peter Pan is an older child, the leader of the Lost<a href="#" class="jsShow showAction">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">In the Little White Bird Peter Pan is a week old baby who lives with fairies. In Peter Pan itself, Peter Pan is an older child, the leader of the Lost Boys who ran away from his family. Now he can't grow up.<a href="#" class="jsHide hideAction">(less)</a></span></span>

<div class="communityAnswerBody">
<div class="answererUserIcon">
<a href="/user/show/4367283-katie" class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto"><img src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/users/1443920667i/4367283._UX100_CR0,38,100,100_.jpg" alt="" /></a>

<div class="communityAnswerTextContainer">
<span class="communityAnswererName">
<a href="/user/show/4367283-katie">Katie</a>
</span>
<span class="communityAnswerText">
<div class="spoiler">
This answer contains spoilers…
<span class="spoilerAnswerText">
<a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span style="display: none" class="spoilerContainer">[
    While the lost boys are boys that fell out of their prams, it is highly unlikely that they are dead, for a few of reasons: <br /><br />1. Falling out of a pram<a rel="nofollow" href="#">…more</a>
    While the lost boys are boys that fell out of their prams, it is highly unlikely that they are dead, for a few of reasons: <br /><br />1. Falling out of a pram would likely cause some bumps and bruises, but is not a cause of instant death for a small child. <br /><br />2. The lost boys can indeed die while in Neverland, which would be hard to explain if they were already dead.<br /><br />3. The lost boys come back to the mainland (AKA the real world) with Wendy, Jon, and Michael, and are adopted by the Darlings. They then grow up into regular ol' adults. <a rel="nofollow" href="#">(less)</a>
  
 <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]-->
</span>

    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_68402182">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/68402182" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/2156200-k-d-absolutely" class="left imgcol" title="K.D. Absolutely"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1449978413p2/2156200.jpg" alt="K.D. Absolutely" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/68402182?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Nov 06, 2010</a>

      <a href="/user/show/2156200-k-d-absolutely" class="user" title="K.D. Absolutely">K.D. Absolutely</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/153857.Peter_Pan_" title="Hardcover 9780743214490" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to K.D. by: </span>
      501 Must Read Books
  </div>



          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/2156200-k-d-absolutely?shelf=501" class="actionLinkLite">501</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/2156200-k-d-absolutely?shelf=childrens" class="actionLinkLite">childrens</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer68402182">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17133671194398100266">A story of a dead child and a mother who is missing him.<br /><br />Sir James Matthew Barrie (1860-1937), a Scottish, wrote this book in 1902 for an older brother, David (his mother's favorite) who died in an ice-skating accident the day before he turned 14. Thus, in his mother's mind, David always stayed as a young boy who would not grow up. J. M. Barrie, a middle-child and then only 6 years old, tried to assume David's place in his mother's heart by wearing the latter's clothes and speaking and sounding l</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText17133671194398100266">A story of a dead child and a mother who is missing him.<br /><br />Sir James Matthew Barrie (1860-1937), a Scottish, wrote this book in 1902 for an older brother, David (his mother's favorite) who died in an ice-skating accident the day before he turned 14. Thus, in his mother's mind, David always stayed as a young boy who would not grow up. J. M. Barrie, a middle-child and then only 6 years old, tried to assume David's place in his mother's heart by wearing the latter's clothes and speaking and sounding like him. Barrie was 42 when <strong>Peter Pan</strong> (the character) first appeared in his other novel, <em>The Little White Bird</em> but the emotion of longing (the child missing his mother and the mother missing his son) can be felt by the readers as if the death only happened recently. For me, this attests to Barrie's brilliance as a novelist.<br /><br />They say that losing one's child is the most painful grief that a parent can have. A parent burying his child is in contradiction to the natural cycle of life. Thus, it is a lifelong journey of grief for the parents. The very young Barrie saw this pain in his mother's heart and so he tried his best to act, speak and sound like his brother. A mother missing her child. In the story this is symbolized by the open bedroom window waiting for Wendy, John and Michael to return. When they finally do, Peter tries closing it but when he sees the tears in Mrs. Darling's eyes, he says <em>"we don't want any silly mothers'"; and he flew away.</em> making it a triumph of a mother's unconditional heart. A child longing for his mother's love. This is symbolized by Peter asking for Wendy to be his mother and probably Tink and probably even Mrs. Darling. This is the moral of the story: we all need mothers especially those whose windows are and will always be open for us.<br /><br />A beautiful book. Mesmerizing prose. A fantasy adventure children's book on the surface. But a sad emotion-filled story of a mother and her son somewhere inside. It has the ethereal beauty of Antoine de Saint-Exupery's <em>Le Petite Prince</em> and the subtle meaningful cycle-of-life lesson in E. B. White's <em>Charlotte's Web</em>, two of favorite children's books. My only regret is that fathers like me are sidelined. We fathers have hearts too and we would like to be part of that love. Why did Barrie depict Mr. Darling as crazy feeding Nana his medicine and has to sleep in the kernel?<br /><br />You see, my windows are also open.<br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="17133671194398100266">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer33732444">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6575913699587717879">My children wanted to do our read aloud outside this evening. So we went on the patio and I began reading "Peter Pan." I read about how the mermaids would play with the bubbles, but when the children would come they would all disappear, but they would secretly watch. Pretty soon I hear over the fence our 11 year old neighbor boy say, "Is that Peter Pan?" "Yes," I say, "Would you like to come listen?" "I've been listening from here," he says. So I go on and read about Wendy's rule that all the bo</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText6575913699587717879">My children wanted to do our read aloud outside this evening. So we went on the patio and I began reading "Peter Pan." I read about how the mermaids would play with the bubbles, but when the children would come they would all disappear, but they would secretly watch. Pretty soon I hear over the fence our 11 year old neighbor boy say, "Is that Peter Pan?" "Yes," I say, "Would you like to come listen?" "I've been listening from here," he says. So I go on and read about Wendy's rule that all the boys must take a nap after they eat and they are all settled on marooners rock when an eery darkness begins to spread of the lagoon. "Oh, it must be Hook!" and the neighbor is now perched on top of the fence. We go on to the fight and Peter is wounded and can't fly nor swim and is left with Wendy on the rock and the tide is coming in. "Oh...but they can't really drown. They don't drown. Do they?" And the boy is now over the fence. There is a kite, Peter fastens Wendy to it and it carries her away. Peter looks out bravely and says 'to die will be an awfully big adventure.' The chapter ends and the neighbor boy is beside us. I smile and ask, "Do you guys want another chapter?" "Well, if you want to," says the boy and so there is a devoted neverbird, a mother sitting on her nest that has fallen out of the tree and is now bobbing up and down in the gentley lapping waters of the lagoon...</span>

        <a href="/review/show/41666680?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jan 13, 2009</a>

      <a href="/user/show/223837-nikki" class="user" title="Nikki">Nikki</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/700817.Peter_Pan" title="Paperback 9780140621419" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/223837-nikki?shelf=classics" class="actionLinkLite">classics</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/223837-nikki?shelf=children-s-and-ya" class="actionLinkLite">children-s-and-ya</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer41666680">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11676850585751123184">I can't believe I've never actually read Peter Pan until now. I'd seen the Disney version, but this is both more charming and more sinister than that. There are lots of sweet little details, like mothers tidying up their children's thoughts, and the kiss on the corner of Mrs Darling's mouth. <br /><br />But Peter is a monstrous sort of figure when you get past the romance of Neverland. He's a wild boy, selfish and cocky. Instead of being a kind of example of innocent childhood, he almost brings to mind the</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText11676850585751123184">I can't believe I've never actually read Peter Pan until now. I'd seen the Disney version, but this is both more charming and more sinister than that. There are lots of sweet little details, like mothers tidying up their children's thoughts, and the kiss on the corner of Mrs Darling's mouth. <br /><br />But Peter is a monstrous sort of figure when you get past the romance of Neverland. He's a wild boy, selfish and cocky. Instead of being a kind of example of innocent childhood, he almost brings to mind the boys from Lord of the Flies. Near the end, it says that he nearly stabs Wendy's baby! And he steals other children.<br /><br />Of course, the moral of the story is that children need mothers. It's just charming enough to get away with the moralising.</span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_31240698">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/31240698" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1008630-andrew" class="left imgcol" title="Andrew"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1208982906p2/1008630.jpg" alt="Andrew" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/31240698?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Mar 28, 2009</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1008630-andrew" class="user" title="Andrew">Andrew</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/34268.Peter_Pan" title="Hardcover 9780805072457" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/1008630-andrew?shelf=book-club" class="actionLinkLite">book-club</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1008630-andrew?shelf=fantasy" class="actionLinkLite">fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer31240698">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10189219840743378223">I was surprised by this book in many good ways. I was expecting something that glorified the Child and its imagination, and perhaps cursed the unstoppable destruction of our Childinity. I was surprised to see this was not truly so. Barrie loves the Child, but he does not hide its foolishness, its selfishness, its ignorance. The Child in this is almost pre-moral. They have some understanding of villainy, but do not grasp the virtue of a hero. Barrie deems a key attribute to being a child as being</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText10189219840743378223">I was surprised by this book in many good ways. I was expecting something that glorified the Child and its imagination, and perhaps cursed the unstoppable destruction of our Childinity. I was surprised to see this was not truly so. Barrie loves the Child, but he does not hide its foolishness, its selfishness, its ignorance. The Child in this is almost pre-moral. They have some understanding of villainy, but do not grasp the virtue of a hero. Barrie deems a key attribute to being a child as being heartless. All of this raises many points for potential argument and discussion and this is why this book so impressed me. Because I am still thinking about it. <br /><br />It is interesting to think that Barrie was mostly a playwright because I highly enjoyed the playful narrative voice he employed and would mark it as one of my key enjoyments for the book. I love a remarkable narrative voice, though I can't say it is entirely unique. However I may be judging it by its followers as opposed to its peers or master. Regardless, I was entertained and thus I applaud.<br /><br />It should be noted that the sensibilities of this book are not those of today's children entertainment. Most notably, Barrie does not sidestep the fact that the lost boys actively seek to kill pirates. This goes back again to one of my earlier comments, but it may seriously affect the reader. This could bring up many discussions of its own: the place of killing in the imagination of a child, how a child views the struggle between good and evil when they perhaps can't properly discern the two, the distinction between our modern censorship and older standards and they why of all of that, and plenty of other things. However if a parent is looking for a book to read to their child, I just figured this should be brought up.<br /><br /><em>As an aside: for those who know the premise behind the comic series Fables, the writer originally intended for Peter Pan to be the Adversary only to find out that they didn't have rights to the character if they published in the UK. After reading this book I can totally see the interpretation he was going with. It makes for a fun idea. He would have obviously had to constructed the means of the Adversary differently, but Pan as a villain is not unbelievable after this book.</em></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="10189219840743378223">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <a href="/user/show/1022982-brad" class="user" title="Brad">Brad</a>

        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="did not like it">did not like it</span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/34268.Peter_Pan" title="Hardcover 9780805072457" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/1022982-brad?shelf=children" class="actionLinkLite">children</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1022982-brad?shelf=better-as-a-movie" class="actionLinkLite">better-as-a-movie</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer23733738">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18149027057543855237">I am not sure I can see why <a title="Peter Pan" href="https://www.goodreads.com/search/search?q=Peter%20Pan">Peter Pan</a> is such a beloved "classic." <a title="J.M. Barrie's" href="https://www.goodreads.com/search/search?q=J.M.%20Barrie%27s">J.M. Barrie's</a> story of the boy who wouldn't grow up just didn't reach me. And I read it aloud to 4 year old boy-girl twins.<br /><br />Oh, they enjoyed it, and I may have bred a love for the story in them that will last (which could be exactly why the story has endured -- parental readings), but no matter how much they liked <a title="Peter Pan" href="https://www.goodreads.com/search/search?q=Peter%20Pan">Peter Pan</a> I could not see the appeal.<br /><br />Wendy drove me crazy; Peter grew increasingly annoying; Hook bored me stiff; ther</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText18149027057543855237">I am not sure I can see why <a title="Peter Pan" href="https://www.goodreads.com/search/search?q=Peter%20Pan">Peter Pan</a> is such a beloved "classic." <a title="J.M. Barrie's" href="https://www.goodreads.com/search/search?q=J.M.%20Barrie%27s">J.M. Barrie's</a> story of the boy who wouldn't grow up just didn't reach me. And I read it aloud to 4 year old boy-girl twins.<br /><br />Oh, they enjoyed it, and I may have bred a love for the story in them that will last (which could be exactly why the story has endured -- parental readings), but no matter how much they liked <a title="Peter Pan" href="https://www.goodreads.com/search/search?q=Peter%20Pan">Peter Pan</a> I could not see the appeal.<br /><br />Wendy drove me crazy; Peter grew increasingly annoying; Hook bored me stiff; there was too much violence; Barrie's narrative interjections grew to be too intrusive; and I generally felt a distinct lack of fun. About the only thing I liked about the book, besides it ending, was Tinkerbell. Her snooty fairy arrogance always made me smile.<br /><br />I know I will incur the wrath of many when I say this, but I actually prefer the Disney version. Walt brought some real joy to the story, and while I will never read <a title="Peter Pan" href="https://www.goodreads.com/search/search?q=Peter%20Pan">Peter Pan</a> again, I will watch the movie. Probably tomorrow.<br /><br />If there wasn't a successful play of Pan I would put the longevity of Barrie's story on the head of Disney. Too bad I can't, but then he's been blamed for enough over the years, hasn't he?</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="18149027057543855237">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_1038365590">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1038365590" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/19590155-vane-j" class="left imgcol" title="Vane J."><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1443339219p2/19590155.jpg" alt="Vane J." /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1038365590?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Dec 12, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/19590155-vane-j" class="user" title="Vane J.">Vane J.</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/34268.Peter_Pan" title="Hardcover 9780805072457" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/19590155-vane-j?shelf=fantasy" class="actionLinkLite">fantasy</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/19590155-vane-j?shelf=children" class="actionLinkLite">children</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/19590155-vane-j?shelf=2015" class="actionLinkLite">2015</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/19590155-vane-j?shelf=adventure" class="actionLinkLite">adventure</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1038365590">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14496886434652952987"><a rel="nofollow" href="https://bookswithchemistry.wordpress.com/"> <img width="350" height="117" class="escapedImg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1433653616i/15127136._SX540_.png" /></a><br /><br /><blockquote>“All children, except one, grow up”</blockquote><br /><br />When I was a kid, I used to think Peter Pan was fantastic. He didn't grow and could do everything he wanted without parents scolding him. After some years I started to be more wary about him. I didn't like how reckless he was and how he lured little children out of their beds. That was my conception of him before starting this book. And indeed, he was a bit like that.<br /><br />You see, the Disney movie isn't really that far from the original story. The difference is tha</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText14496886434652952987"><a rel="nofollow" href="https://bookswithchemistry.wordpress.com/">
  <img width="350" height="117" class="escapedImg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1433653616i/15127136._SX540_.png" /></a><br /><br /><blockquote>“All children, except one, grow up”</blockquote><br /><br />When I was a kid, I used to think Peter Pan was fantastic. He didn't grow and could do everything he wanted without parents scolding him. After some years I started to be more wary about him. I didn't like how reckless he was and how he lured little children out of their beds. That was my conception of him before starting this book. And indeed, he was a bit like that.<br /><br />You see, the Disney movie isn't really that far from the original story. The difference is that between reading and watching, I think the first one got me better. Let's take a look at the things Peter does, and tell me if he really seems like an angel:<br /><br />• <strong>Steals food from birds to eat it.</strong><br /><br /><blockquote>“I nipped a bit out of that eagle's mouth pretty neatly, Wendy”</blockquote><br /><br />But you know how he does it? By pursuing the bird and getting from their mouths what they have inside and eating it. Charming, huh?<br /><br />• <strong>Thinks it's funny to play with human life.</strong><br /><br />At one point, Michael was tired and he fell from the sky. This is what Peter did:<br /><br /><blockquote>“but he always waited till the last moment, and you felt it was his cleverness that interested him and not the saving of human life”</blockquote><br /><br />It was kind of scary that he thought that way of the life of a baby. Later, though, this was said, and it disturbed me:<br /><br /><blockquote>“Also he was fond of variety, and the sport that engrossed him one moment would suddenly cease to engage him, so there was always the possibility that the next time you fell he would let you go.”</blockquote><br /><br />• <strong>Killing is a game for him.</strong><br /><br /><blockquote>“There's a pirate asleep in the pampas just beneath us," Peter told him. "If you like, we'll go down and kill him.”</blockquote><br /><br />• <strong>He was kind of... tyrannical.</strong><br /><br /><blockquote>“Peter never quite knew what twins were, and his band were not allowed to know anything he did not know”</blockquote><br /><br />No one is allowed to know anything he doesn't. How... wonderful.<br /><br />...<br /><br />But he wasn't all that bad. There was some innocence in him, and he made me a little sad at times because all he wanted was a mother, even though he didn't want to admit it.<br /><br />The innocence of the other children was quite sweet too. However, this was no perfect book. I will show you what bothered me by means of an example:<br /><br /><blockquote>(...)“and while he slept, Wendy and John and Michael flew into the room” (...) “but something must have happened since then, for it is not they who have flown in, it is Peter and Tinker Bell.”</blockquote><br /><br />If it were a 1st person narrator, I would have forgiven it, but it was omniscient, and he spoke from the past, so he was supposed to know everything. Then, why bother saying something and then correcting himself later? It was fine that he did it once, yet it happened lots of times, and it was very annoying.<br /><br />Still, the book in general was cute, entertaining, sometimes disturbing and exasperating because of Peter, but always one I recommend. If you have already read this, by the way, I recommend as a complement The Child Thief by Brom, which is a dark (and very good) retelling of Peter Pan.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="14496886434652952987">...more</a>

          </span>

  <a href="/user/show/874283-janene" class="left imgcol" title="Janene"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1238472856p2/874283.jpg" alt="Janene" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/18896028?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Apr 10, 2008</a>

      <a href="/user/show/874283-janene" class="user" title="Janene">Janene</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/1628260.Peter_Pan" title=" 9781566197137" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/874283-janene?shelf=picture-book" class="actionLinkLite">picture-book</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer18896028">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16411300324371037258">This was such a treat! Three things: 1. It made me realize what a perfect Pan-type Peter I married, so many similarities, some that made me laugh out loud. 2. It made me want to look into my 4-yr-old's imaginitive eyes a little longer. 3. I also occasionally picked up my 20-month-old while sleeping just to rock and enjoy him for extra minutes. <br /><br />This book just so fully captures childhood and the problem of growing up, in a witty way. If you've never read it, really you must! The edition we own is</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText16411300324371037258">This was such a treat! Three things: 1. It made me realize what a perfect Pan-type Peter I married, so many similarities, some that made me laugh out loud. 2. It made me want to look into my 4-yr-old's imaginitive eyes a little longer. 3. I also occasionally picked up my 20-month-old while sleeping just to rock and enjoy him for extra minutes. <br /><br />This book just so fully captures childhood and the problem of growing up, in a witty way. If you've never read it, really you must! The edition we own is spectacular -- the illustrations by Scott Gustafson are beyond compare.<br /><br />Some favorite quotes:<br /><br />"Mrs. Darling first heard of Peter when she was tidying up her children's minds. It is the nightly custom of every good mother after her children are asleep to rummage in their minds and put things straight for next morning, repacking into their proper places the many articles that have wandered during the day. If you could keep awake (but of course you can't) you would see your own mother doing this, and you would find it very interesting to watch her. It is quite like tidying up drawers. You would see her on her knees, I expect, lingering humorously over some of your contents, wondering where on earth you had picked this thing up, making discoveries sweet and not so sweet, pressing this to her cheek as if it were as nice as a kitten, and hurriedly stowing that out of sight. When you wake in the morning, the naughtinesses and evil passions with which you went to bed have been folded up small and placed at the bottom of your mind; and on the top, beautifully aired, are spread out your prettier thoughts, ready for you to put on."<br /><br />"The difference between him and the other boys at such a time was that they knew it was make-believe, while to him make-believe and true were exactly the same thing. This sometimes troubled them, as when they had to make-believe that they had had their dinners." </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="16411300324371037258">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer11326564647759716636">This is my favorite book of all time. When you grow up with the "overly-nice" Disney version of the story, picking up this book for the first time can be quite a shock.   <span style="display:none" id="freeText11326564647759716636">This is my favorite book of all time. When you grow up with the "overly-nice" Disney version of the story, picking up this book for the first time can be quite a shock. The book will also shatter the image that most girls have of Tinkerbell but personally-I prefer the original. Johnny Corkscrew, Peter's idea of a kiss, sewing on a shadow, sifting through the thoughts of your children as they sleep... So many things about this book are missed by those who never bother to pick it up because they "know the story". Peter Pan is not an innocent child, he has experienced more as a child than most of us do in our entire lives... The best moment, the best quote of this book is when Peter says, "To die will be an awfully big adventure." We should all view life, and death, through the eyes of Peter Pan..</span>

And to go to Neverland.<br /><br /> Love, Audrey"</strong><br /><br /><img width="500" height="375" class="escapedImg" alt="description" src="https://d.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1381009787ra/3803687.gif" /><br /><br /><strong>"To die will be an awfully big adventure."</strong><br /><br /> When you hear the name Peter Pan and Wendy, what does it remind you of? It is beautiful and magical.The writing is amazing and it is so easy to understand. It makes you feel like you are living in the book and you are either: the Lost Boy</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText3029989099773780018"> <strong>"Dear Peter Pan, <br /><br /> What I would give to fly away with you! And to go to Neverland.<br /><br /> Love, Audrey"</strong><br /><br /><img width="500" height="375" class="escapedImg" alt="description" src="https://d.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1381009787ra/3803687.gif" /><br /><br /><strong>"To die will be an awfully big adventure."</strong><br /><br /> When you hear the name Peter Pan and Wendy, what does it remind you of? Happiness, childhood, innocence, flying away, love, and so much more right? <br /><br /> That's exactly what this book makes you feel. It is beautiful and magical.The writing is amazing and it is so easy to understand. It makes you feel like you are living in the book and you are either: the Lost Boys, Hook, Peter, Wendy, Smee, or <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/465772212">Tiger Lily</a>(to be honest I didn't even know she was a real character in the book. I thought Jodi Anderson created her. What a shame right?)<br /><br /> Peter Pan has been going on for ages and since I can remember. I think I fell in love with Peter(shh, I think most of us has right? And I am not ashamed to admit it!) I think you are never to young for Peter Pan. <br /><br /><img width="500" height="375" class="escapedImg" alt="description" src="https://d.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1381009787ra/3803688.gif" /><br /><br /> Peter Pan and Wendy is a quick read and its about Peter Pan and how he came to meet Wendy, John, and Michael. As we all know he jumps into their room looking for his shadow. Wendy wakes up and ask:<br /><strong>"Why are you crying boy?"</strong><br /><br /><img width="500" height="240" class="escapedImg" alt="description" src="https://d.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1381010546ra/3807353.gif" /><br /><br />and thus the adventure begins.<br /><br /><img width="500" height="247" class="escapedImg" alt="description" src="https://d.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1381009787ra/3803690.gif" /><br /><br /> The adventures of swimming with mermaids, fighting pirates and meeting red skins and odd animals. <br /><br /><img width="500" height="600" class="escapedImg" alt="description" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1381009787i/3803691._SY540_.png" /><br /><br /><strong>“All the world is made of faith, and trust, and pixie dust.” </strong><br /><br />The adventures of meeting Tinker Bell and dealing with her. <br /><br /><br />And finally the sad decision.<br /><br /><img width="496" height="280" class="escapedImg" alt="description" src="https://d.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1381009787ra/3803692.gif" /><br /><br /> ********<br /><br /><img width="350" height="263" class="escapedImg" alt="description" src="https://d.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1381009787ra/3803693.gif" /><br /><br /><img width="500" height="227" class="escapedImg" alt="description" src="https://d.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1380753037ra/3119758.gif" /><br /><br />******<br /><br />Peter Pan is a beautiful story. I loved it and J.M. Barrie was a genius. <br /><br /> I think that in order for you to truly read this and enjoy this you just have to believe and love everything about Peter Pan. I recommend this to everybody!<br /><br /><img width="500" height="211" class="escapedImg" alt="description" src="https://d.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1381009787ra/3803694.gif" /><br /><br /><strong>“Just always be waiting for me.” </strong><br /><br /><img width="500" height="238" class="escapedImg" alt="description" src="https://d.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1381009787ra/3803695.gif" /><br /><br /><br /><a rel="nofollow" href="http://audreyhepburnbooks.wordpress.com/2013/10/05/peter-pan-and-wendy-by-j-m-barrie-review/">See it at THE READING GIRL</a><br /></span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_508920021">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/508920021" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/2639302-steph" class="left imgcol" title="Steph"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1342983996p2/2639302.jpg" alt="Steph" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/508920021?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">May 10, 2013</a>

      <a href="/user/show/2639302-steph" class="user" title="Steph">Steph</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="did not like it">did not like it</span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/290131.Peter_Pan_and_Wendy" title=" 9780543949790" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer508920021">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1065145398004178948">Firstly, let me make it clear that there is actually more than one J M Barrie 'Peter Pan' story (something that I did not initially realise). There is 'Peter Pan and Wendy,' which is the story we are all familiar with (immortalised - inaccurately - by Disney); there is 'Peter Pan in Kensington Gardens,' which tells the story of him as a baby with the lost boys when he was originally abandoned, (which I have not yet read) and then 'The Little White Bird' (which I have not read either), but is a s</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText1065145398004178948">Firstly, let me make it clear that there is actually more than one J M Barrie 'Peter Pan' story (something that I did not initially realise). There is 'Peter Pan and Wendy,' which is the story we are all familiar with (immortalised - inaccurately - by Disney); there is 'Peter Pan in Kensington Gardens,' which tells the story of him as a baby with the lost boys when he was originally abandoned, (which I have not yet read) and then 'The Little White Bird' (which I have not read either), but is a set of stories in which Peter also appears. However, this is the most famous of the narratives and I read it mainly because I was looking for famous pirate descriptions for a scheme of work I wanted to teach to my Year 7s. I know, I am very dull! <br /><br />So...the story is pretty much what one would expect if you have encountered Peter in his many big screen incarnations: Peter encourages Wendy, John and Michael to fly off to Neverland to the Lost Boys. Wendy becomes their surrogate mother and they undertake a whole series of adventures together until they begin to miss home. Of course, there are the famous faces: Captain Hook who captures them all and is always looking to gain revenge on Peter; Tinkerbell who adores Peter and is reliant on the clapping of children to survive; Tiger Lily and her deadly crew and of course the Darling family waiting patiently and desperately at home while their children undertake the adventure of a lifetime.<br /><br />Oh how lovely? What a smashing tale for children? What a sweet little narrative about childhood and innocence? NO! NO! NO! NO! This was a highly disturbing and even distasteful children's tale in my opinion and I can only assume that the glowing reviews it seems to receive are based on the fact that too often we read this book wearing Disney blinkers, seeing what we want to and not recognising its dark underbelly. So what were my problems? (I'm afraid that there will have to be a list as there are simply too many):<br /><br />1. The obvious violence delivered callously and without remorse throughout (I have no issue with violence, but not in a young children's narrative)<br /><br />2. Peter's characterisation - there is nothing appealing about this arrogant, deceitful, manipulative imp, who cares for little but himself and this never changes! <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span style="display: none" class="spoilerContainer">[SPOILER: Tinkerbell nearly dies and is devoted to Peter, but a year later she has vanished and he can't even be bothered to remember her name. This is a sadistic bully who becomes infuriated when his domination of the lost boys fails and is quite content to undertake violence simply for the pleasure of doing so. He is quite content to take Wendy, John and Michael from their families knowing how much the others yearn for a mother and that in the process, they will lose theirs. He even refuses to allow the Lost Boys to talk about their lost mothers in order to maintain his hold over them and his lie that all mothers are silly and horrid creatures. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />3. The novel begins with Mrs Darling rummaging around in her children's BRAINS...yes BRAINS...to tidy up their minds! Barrie describes: 'Mrs. Darling first heard of Peter when she was tidying up her children's minds. It is the nightly custom of every good mother after her children are asleep to rummage in their minds and put things straight for next morning, repacking into their proper places the many articles that have wandered during the day. If you could keep awake (but of course you can't) you would see your own mother doing this, and you would find it very interesting to watch her. It is quite like tidying up drawers' The implication being she is sorting out their thoughts and removing anything unsuitable like some form of Edwardian brainwashing!<br /><br />4. The portrayal of Mr Darling who can only be described as an absolute buffoon. He is an aggressive and cruel man who abuses the lovely dog Nana feeding her his medicine because he is too cowardly to take it himself and it is this that results in his children being abducted. The implication is that he is a clock watcher who wants an easy life and that his wife has had to manipulate him and her menstrual cycle in order to conceive the second two children that he never wanted! However, when the children vanish, his behaviour can only be classified as bizarre: he sleeps in a kennel even going to work with it as penance. Ludicrous!<br /><br />5. At one point, Barry describes how the fairies return through the forest 'FROM AN ORGY!' And I quote: 'After a time he fell asleep, and some unsteady fairies had to climb over him on their way home from an orgy'. Surely inappropriate and peculiar in a children's novel.<br /><br />6. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span style="display: none" class="spoilerContainer">[SPOILER: At the end of the novel, we are told that Wendy's ancestor's return to Peter (when he bothers to remember them, such is his egotism) to do his spring cleaning on a yearly basis! Well, what a mother you have become Wendy! What mother would allow their child to return to place of danger and death with some a mendacious creature as Peter knowing there was no way to access them again. Moreover, the implication seems to be that, this is all such women are good for: cleaning! <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />7. In fact, the novel is obviously misogynistic throughout. The message is that all mothers fail and are essentially useless creatures. Peter hates mothers (although his reason is that she abanadoned him, but Barrie seems to blame her for giving up hope and barring the window and having another child after years of his absence, implying that she should have lived eternally with the guilt and blame of his loss and never moved on). At one point we are told, 'mothers alone are always willing to be the buffer' implying it is perfectly acceptable to blame them for everything. Likewise, Mrs Darling, who seems a fairly caring, attentive if vacuous woman, is blamed for the loss of her children. Barrie tells us 'so long as mothers are like this, their children will take advantage of them' so clearly generosity of spirit and loving your children, is a terrible flaw in woman kind. Moreover, the sole function of Wendy's descendants is to become Peter's mother in turn! In short, this is a novel that presents women as useless creatures whose only use is for cleaning, breeding and raising little brats. The only other female figures presented are in the mode of the femme fatale - they seem deceptive, cruel and use their physical attraction to manipulate men such as Tiger Lily who uses her physical charms to rule the Indians and Tinkerbell, who out of female jealousy, gets the Lost Boys to shoot Wendy. So what Barrie presents us with is the disturbing Victorian dichotomy that women are either the angel in the house of the whore in the street.<br />8. Having said this, Barrie actually appears misanthropic as well. He has little good to say about anyone (least of all children).<br /><br />Ironically, the one redeeming feature of this novel is that all the proceeds went to Great Ormond Street Hospital to help fund cures for children's genetic diseases. And for that one admirable action, I give this book a lonely star.<br /><br />Because, in short, this is a terribly disturbing book, written by a terribly disturbing mind - if only his mother had 'tidied it up'!<!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="1065145398004178948">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<span id="freeTextContainer7884489409453171617">All these years I've travelled far away from the Peter Pan phenomenon, from Disney to the numerous movies inspired by the novel (the only thing I remember is that great attraction in Disneyland Paris where you're surrounded by stars and you fly in the Jolly Roger - but I think I digress). I still don't understand how I could have avoided such a masterpiece at school, or how nobody ever told me before to put down my cartoons and go read something that important. <br /><br />Everything involved here is so bea</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText7884489409453171617">All these years I've travelled far away from the Peter Pan phenomenon, from Disney to the numerous movies inspired by the novel (the only thing I remember is that great attraction in Disneyland Paris where you're surrounded by stars and you fly in the Jolly Roger - but I think I digress). I still don't understand how I could have avoided such a masterpiece at school, or how nobody ever told me before to put down my cartoons and go read something that important. <br /><br />Everything involved here is so beautifuly approached that I'm still emotional trying to explain myself. This is the kind of story which can follow you decades after, not only thanks to the writers' extraordinary writing style but also because of its poetry and the scope of its morality.<br /><br /><blockquote>
  <em>
    <strong>“Never say goodbye because goodbye means going away and going away means forgetting.”</strong>
  </em>
</blockquote><br /><br />Last thing but not the worst one, I ship so much Peter and Wendy. Just. Yes.<br /><br /><br /><img width="auto" height="auto" class="escapedImg" alt="peter &amp; wendy" src="https://d.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1451962577ra/17626461.gif" /><br /><br /></span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_1455233066">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1455233066" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/4508542-mark-lawrence" class="left imgcol" title="Mark Lawrence"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1442536170p2/4508542.jpg" alt="Mark Lawrence" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1455233066?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Nov 29, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/4508542-mark-lawrence" class="user" title="Mark Lawrence">Mark Lawrence</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/34268.Peter_Pan" title="Hardcover 9780805072457" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1455233066">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1012472018694580898">I read this to Celyn. It's a short book. Google tells me 47,000 words but it felt shorter than that.<br /><br />Many of us know the story second hand through cartoons, Hollywood adaptations, and picture-books. The original item is not that dissimilar, though it's a fair bit more brutal that the cartoons and having been published in 1911 it's 100 years out of date when it comes to Native Americans!<br /><br />The first thing to note is that it's not just the Never-Land that has a surreal, imaginary feel to it. The Darli</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText1012472018694580898">I read this to Celyn. It's a short book. Google tells me 47,000 words but it felt shorter than that.<br /><br />Many of us know the story second hand through cartoons, Hollywood adaptations, and picture-books. The original item is not that dissimilar, though it's a fair bit more brutal that the cartoons and having been published in 1911 it's 100 years out of date when it comes to Native Americans!<br /><br />The first thing to note is that it's not just the Never-Land that has a surreal, imaginary feel to it. The Darlings's home life is rather odd, with a dog acting as nursemaid to the three little Darlings, performing such tasks as getting them dressed, bathing them, and giving them medicine...<br /><br />I liked the imagination on display where Peter Pan's shadow is torn off as he leaves in a hurry and the children's mother rolls it up and stores it in a drawer. Later Wendy sews it back on.<br /><br />The main difference is in how callous Peter Pan is, and how he stays true to this self-absorbed character the whole time with no softening. He doesn't give a damn about the Lost Boys or Wendy's brothers. Tinkerbell is likewise remorseless, repeatedly attempting to get Wendy not just sent home but actively killed.<br /><br />In the battles the boys have knives and use them to kill people. It's all in the bang-bang-you're-dead vibe of children's games, but the fact remains that pretty much every person on the Never-Land island is killed with violence by the end of the book. This includes most of the Redskins (whose portrayal in the manner given here would fall south of the racist-border in any of the last 4 decades), and pretty much all of the pirates. Even Wendy gets shot with an arrow at Tinkerbell's behest, though she turns out to be alright due to some rather hard to visualize complication with an acorn.<br /><br />One surprise for me was that Captain Jas. Hook appears to be our pirate captain's real and longstanding name. The fact he now has a hook for a hand being pure coincidence!<br /><br />Anyway - the book is full of good things, from the ticking crocodile to the invention of the Wendy House.<br /><br />And Peter Pan, true to his word, never grows up. True to his character he soon forgets about Wendy, returning many years later and fixing his attentions to her daughter, and later granddaughter.<br /><br />I'm withholding the 5th star simply because much of the description is rather vague, summary, implausible or all three together, so it can be hard to visualize/believe in the scenes. <br /><br /><br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="1012472018694580898">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <a href="/user/show/19300421-jean" class="user" title="Jean">Jean</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/34268.Peter_Pan" title="Hardcover 9780805072457" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/19300421-jean?shelf=read-authors-a-b" class="actionLinkLite">read-authors-a-b</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer651438821">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer35194095364522751">This edition of <strong>Peter Pan</strong> contains the text of J.M. Barrie's 1911 novel, <em>"Peter and Wendy"</em>, which he wrote from his earlier play of 1904. The character of Peter Pan, the little boy who wouldn't grow up, had already made an appearance in an earlier work by J.M. Barrie, <em>"The Little White Bird"</em> (1902). There continue to be many retellings of this magical story, and Peter is himself a timeless figure; one of the best-loved characters in children's literature. There is maybe a little of Peter in ever</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText35194095364522751">This edition of <strong>Peter Pan</strong> contains the text of J.M. Barrie's 1911 novel, <em>"Peter and Wendy"</em>, which he wrote from his earlier play of 1904. The character of Peter Pan, the little boy who wouldn't grow up, had already made an appearance in an earlier work by J.M. Barrie, <em>"The Little White Bird"</em> (1902). There continue to be many retellings of this magical story, and Peter is himself a timeless figure; one of the best-loved characters in children's literature. There is maybe a little of Peter in everybody. We can all empathise with that concept; it speaks to our inner psyche.<br /><br />But what are we to make of the original? For any readers critical of modern children's fiction for being too violent, I would direct them to read this piece (plus some Lewis Carroll, and <em>"Strewelpeter…"</em>) to see what was considered appropriate for Victorian children. It is by turns overblown, full of Victorian sentiment and whimsy, but there is also a dark side with very grim overtones. There is betrayal, selfishness, cruelty, torture and bloodthirstiness galore. For, <br /><br /><em>"children are ever ready, when novelty knocks, to desert their dearest ones."</em><br /><br />William Golding's <em>"Lord of the Flies"</em> owes a lot to this book. And it is not only the children and the "baddies" who are depicted as evil and malicious. Their parents seem full of hypocrisy too. <br /><br />For instance, a few pages into the story, the Darlings are discussing whether or not they can afford to keep their newborn baby, Wendy. Then a little later there is a "competition" between father and son about who will take his medicine more bravely. The father pours his medicine into the dog's bowl and tricks her into drinking it. He treats this as a great joke although the rest of the family do not think so. What is the message here? Parents betray you? Parents do not feel remorse? Or is it simply very black humour? The dog "Nana", incidentally, is just that. She is quite literally, a nursemaid to the children. Whimsy? Humour? A little of both probably, although I do remember finding this confusing myself, as a child.<br /><br />A further observation on how traitorous adults can be comes later in the story, when Hook bites Peter as he is helping him up,<br /><br /><em>"its unfairness was what dazed Peter… He could only stare horrified. Every child is affected thus the first time he is treated unfairly… After you have been unfair to him he will love you again, but he will never afterwards be the same boy."</em><br /><br />The story of <strong>Peter Pan</strong> is the stuff of dreams. Or is it? Isn't it more the stuff of nightmares? Look at the pirates. There is the cadaverous Captain Hook with his Charles II costume and of course the murderous hook instead of a hand. He is tormented by the thought of the crocodile which pursues him - and who has opportunely swallowed an alarm clock to increase Hook's dread. And in addition Hook is oddly scared of the sight of his own blood. Hook is a tormented character, <br /><br /><em>"ever a dark and solitary enigma, he stood aloof from his followers in spirit as in substance."</em> <br /><br />It becomes clear that he was an ex-Etonian, with a sorry past. <br /><br /><em>"Hook was not his real name,"</em> states Barrie.<br /><br />Then there is his second-in-command Smee, who, <em>"had pleasant names for everything, and his cutlass was Johnny Corkscrew because he wriggled it in the wound. One could mention many lovable traits in Smee." </em>The author lurches between sardonic humour such as this, and being curiously dispassionate about the story,<em> "Let us now kill a pirate to show Hook's method. Skylights will do".</em><br /><br />The Lost Boys, although given individual names, again seem to be curiously abstract and interchangeable. Depicted as budding pirates themselves, they, <em>"vary in numbers… they get killed and so on; and when they seem to be growing up, which is against the rules, Peter thins them out."</em> <br /><br />Thins them out?! <br /><br />He also hunts down Captain Hook, while he, <em>"swore a terrible oath: "Hook or me this time."</em> He crawled forward like a snake with, <em>"one finger on his lip and his dagger at the ready. He was frightfully happy." </em><br /><br />Yes, Peter could be said to be the most merciless character of them all. But Barrie depicts him as truly amoral, perpetually in that very early stage of childhood where "the self" is the centre of the universe.<br /><br /><em>"The difference between him and the other boys at such a time was that they knew it was make-believe, while to him make-believe and true were exactly the same thing." </em><br /><br />The character of Peter is consistent with this throughout. He frequently forgets things - and people - and views his own actions as responsible for anything which pleases him. Thus his <em>"crowing"</em>. Barrie has given us a perfect description of a child's focus prior to learning about others, or such concepts as responsibility, cause and effect. It is merely the reader's interpretation to regard him as a "mischievous boy". The character himself is a long way off such self-knowledge.<br /><br />The idea of <em>"Neverland"</em> is an intriguing one. Again, it speaks to something deep inside us all. The three children found that they recognised the island from their dreams. It had aspects of all they desired, and also much of what they feared. It was different for each, and yet the same. It was make-believe, but also with real threats. This dual perception of reality is a constant theme throughout the novel, and very hard to grasp. "It doesn't matter, it's only make-believe", we think. And then, "Oh no, but it's not!" At one point Peter,<br /><br /><em>"regretted that he had given the birds of the island such strange names and that they are very wild and difficult of approach."</em> <br /><br />The Lost Boys are variously acting as redskins or pirates, switching at will. Barrie's skill at depicting how involved they become in their characters adds to the blurring of unrealities.<br /><br />There is no doubt that Barrie's imaginative and inventive powers are superb. "Tinkerbell", the selfish fairy, is another whose persona has seeped into the public's consciousness. <br /><br /><em>"Tink was not all bad: or, rather, she was all bad just now, but, on the other hand, sometimes she was all good." </em><br /><br />Interestingly, the use of <em>"fairy dust"</em> to enable the children to fly is a later addition. After the stage play, parents had complained to Barrie that their children were hurting themselves by jumping out of their beds and "trying to fly". This seems an extraordinary detail for Barrie to believe necessitated changing in such a bloodthirsty tale!<br /><br />Actually, Barrie has slotted into a common traditional folk view of the little people as being essentially bad. He refers to the fairies coming home <em>"unsteady… from an orgy"</em> the night before, looking for malevolent tricks to play. But Tinkerbell is loyal to Peter throughout, and of course when all the audience (or readers) are urged to clap their hands, or else she will die, this is pure magic. But Peter stays true to character. By the end of the story he does not even remember her. <br /><br /><em>"There are such a lot of them," he said, "I expect she is no more."</em><br /><br />Again, what does this teach a young reader about loyalty or friendship? This is a ruthless tale, not a moral one.<br /><br />If we look for a "good" character, we tend to trip over Wendy, who seems to be an archetype for Barrie's idea of females. She delights in being a "mother" to the lost boys, forgets her true home much as her brothers do, spends all her time cooking, cleaning and darning, and professes to feel sorry for spinsters. The reader doesn't get the impression that this is ironic; more likely, wish-fulfilment on behalf of the author. Even during the bloodbath at the end, she,<br /><br /><em>"praised them all equally and shuddered delightfully when Michael [her youngest brother] showed her the place where he had killed one..."</em><br /><br />A psychologist would have a field day with this book. Indeed, there is a "Peter Pan" syndrome, to describe individuals who are reluctant to take on "adult" cares and responsibilities, preferring to pursue their own, often creative, interests. And there is plenty of substance to support the view that Barrie was a troubled individual, and that this fed into his writing. His elder brother David, died in a tragic skating accident at the age of fourteen. This deeply affected their mother. The dual parallels with the boy who couldn't grow up, and would therefore remain a boy for ever, and the idealised mother, are quite blatant. Then when James Barrie grew up, he apparently had a troubled marriage, with difficulties making love, which alienated his wife. <br /><br />He became close friends with the Llewelyn Davies family, having met two of the boys in Kensington Gardens, and began to tell them stories about his invented character Peter Pan. Barrie coined the name using the first name of one of the five, and "Pan" from the mischievous god of the woodlands. Again, this story is overlaid with sadness. In 1907 the father Arthur died of cancer of the jaw, and three years later the mother Sylvia followed, apparently from lung cancer. Barrie became their guardian in 1910, and from then on even closer to the boys. <br /><br />But the real life tragedies continued. The eldest, George, was killed like much of his generation on Flanders Field in 1915. The character of Peter Pan was apparently primarily based on him. Michael, who was deeply afraid of the water, drowned in 1921 with a classmate at Oxford. And in 1960 Peter, the second son, threw himself in front of a subway train in London.<br /><br />Much has been made of Barrie's interest in these children, just as has been with Lewis Carroll's interest in children, especially in our over-sensitive and suspicious climate. This is a bit of a mystery. Surely an interest in children is natural and common to all humans, to a greater or lesser degree, whether male or female. Would it seem so "shocking" if these two writers had been female? <br /><br />Surely the point is that writers write from their own experience. Even if what they write is ostensibly pure fantasy, there will be facets of their own experience underlying it. Like most writers he took his inspiration from real life and reworked the people he knew and loved to populate his books and plays. Many experiences came together to make James Barrie's creation of an immortal little boy. In some ways he was writing about what he wished might happen. But because of that creation, current history will unfortunately peer into his personal life. He achieved immortality himself, but at a price.<br /><br />So far this has been an analysis of the text of the original novel, which is perhaps rarely read now. Certainly the perception of the story of Peter Pan is a much "softer" version, deduced from a composite number of sources. This edition of the text though, dates from 1987, and was reissued in 2003 as a Centennial Edition (presumably in readiness for 2004, 100 years after the first edition.) It has decorative illustrations by Michael Hague which complement the text perfectly. They are watercolours with a wealth of detail, using subtle colours and complicated patterns which appeal far more to an adult than a child. They are moody and sensitive without being sentimental. And there are a lot of them - between two and four for each of the seventeen chapters. It is a beautiful book.<br /><br />Reading the original <strong>Peter Pan</strong> as an adult has been a startling experience. It is not at all what a reader might expect, and although Barrie wrote it as a children's story, this book as it stands would not appeal to a modern-day child. We have all lost the capacity for appreciating whimsy in the same way. A child might well enjoy the bloodthirsty nature of the book, and the absoluteness of punishment and judgment. There are few shades of grey in this book. Nobody is urged to "get along" with anybody else. And the adults are seriously flawed. But the cosiness of the language makes it an unlikely choice.<br /><br />It does however deserve four stars from an adult's point of view. From its first instantly recognisable line, <br /><br /><em>"All children, except one, grow up"</em><br /><br />through Peter Pan's claim, <br /><br /><em>"To die will be an awfully big adventure,"</em><br /><br />it is an incomparable classic. <br /><br /><em>"Off we skip like the most heartless things in the world, which is what children are…and we have an entirely selfish time, and then when we have need of special attention we nobly return for it, confident that we shall be rewarded instead of smacked" </em><br /><br />observes J.M Barrie. The characters in this book, especially Peter Pan, act out that theory to perfection. The book ends with the phrase, <br /><br /><em>"and so it will go on, as long as children are gay and innocent and heartless." </em><br /><br />For all its flaws it is a unique and truly imaginative book, with an unforgettable antihero, and which has spawned many imitations.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer950184525918703136">I absolutely love the movie <em>Finding Neverland</em> and always felt a bit stupid for not having read the story that inspired it. <em>Peter Pan</em> turned out to be exactly what I thought it would be: A rich and extremely imaginary fairy tale with some surprisingly brutal scenes and questionable morals that definitely have to be seen within its time of creation.<br />--- read for the #5books7days challenge</span>

  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/224835396" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/5645967-laurel" class="left imgcol" title="Laurel"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1442944187p2/5645967.jpg" alt="Laurel" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/224835396?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Nov 29, 2011</a>

      <a href="/user/show/5645967-laurel" class="user" title="Laurel">Laurel</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/700817.Peter_Pan" title="Paperback 9780140621419" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/5645967-laurel?shelf=mermaids" class="actionLinkLite">mermaids</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/5645967-laurel?shelf=favorites" class="actionLinkLite">favorites</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/5645967-laurel?shelf=how-to-make-memorable-characters" class="actionLinkLite">how-to-make-memorable-characters</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/5645967-laurel?shelf=fantasy" class="actionLinkLite">fantasy</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/5645967-laurel?shelf=precious-things" class="actionLinkLite">precious-things</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/5645967-laurel?shelf=illustrated-beauties" class="actionLinkLite">illustrated-beauties</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/5645967-laurel?shelf=short-n-sweet" class="actionLinkLite">short-n-sweet</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/5645967-laurel?shelf=adventure" class="actionLinkLite">adventure</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/5645967-laurel?shelf=badass-kids-taking-names" class="actionLinkLite">badass-kids-taking-names</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/5645967-laurel?shelf=daww" class="actionLinkLite">daww</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/5645967-laurel?shelf=tears-were-shed" class="actionLinkLite">tears-were-shed</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer224835396">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11616159118918038906">“<em>All children, except one, grow up. They soon know that they will grow up, and the way Wendy knew was this. One day when she was two years old she was playing in a garden, and she plucked another flower and ran with it to her mother. I suppose she must have looked rather delightful, for Mrs Darling put her hand to her heart and cried, ‘Oh, why can’t you remain like this for ever!’ This was all that passed between them on the subject, but henceforth Wendy knew that she must grow up. You always kn</em></span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText11616159118918038906">“<em>All children, except one, grow up. They soon know that they will grow up, and the way Wendy knew was this. One day when she was two years old she was playing in a garden, and she plucked another flower and ran with it to her mother. I suppose she must have looked rather delightful, for Mrs Darling put her hand to her heart and cried, ‘Oh, why can’t you remain like this for ever!’ This was all that passed between them on the subject, but henceforth Wendy knew that she must grow up. You always know after you are two. Two is the beginning of the end.</em>”<br /><br />How’s <strong>that</strong> for an opener? Holy hell.<br /><br />This book was pure good.<br />A huge course of emotions is what J.M. Barrie decides to bring to the Classic Literature Dinner Table. It is placed right in between Gaston Leroux’s mask-shaped brie and crackers, and Dumas’s sweet revenge dessert platter.<br /><br />This is a story of magic even more magical that I could even imagine in my wildest dreams. The best part? It’s good-hearted nature and utmost ridiculous explanations of the magic presented made this story one of the best, the elite.<br /><br />We all know Peter Pan, at least, we know Disney’s super duper fluffy version, or maybe even the Jeremy Sumpter version (and personally I can only handle Robin Williams’ version because of Dustin Hoffman....and Robin Williams).<br /><br />Anywho, Peter Pan is a small boy who can never grow up. Why? Maybe it’s because he lives in Neverland, or maybe it’s because his mother forgot him. A boy who became dead to the world he deserted. He takes the Darling children - Wendy, John, and Michael - to the Neverland, where theirs dreams all come together. They fly there using Tink’s fairy dust, to the second star to the right and straight on ‘til morning.<br /><br /><strong>Oh my god this book.</strong><br /><br />J.M. Barrie knew his audience, and he knew how to develop characters before even a word was spoken from their mouths. I’ve yet to see such detail in these characters since the Dursley descriptions in the first Harry Potter book. In one paragraph, I knew everything I could possibly know about Mrs, Darling’s personality, Mr. Darling’s, and their relationship as a couple. Having read through this, I understand why we dove into Mrs. Darling so deeply, but I’m so amazed at how beautifully written this whole story was.<br /><br />It was short and sweet, with many points of laughter, suspense, pride, jealousy, and of course, a giant ball of sadness that it was far too difficult (for me) to get past. Barrie was a genius, a damn smart genius. Who needs scientific logic sometimes? Fairies are born when babies laugh; that’s logical enough for me.<br /><br />Barrie is exceptionally smart about what the story’s about. We follow the tales of Peter through the Darlings, and we know them the best before Peter’s arrival, but it’s obvious that the book is <em>about Peter</em>, and no, we can’t figure that out simply from the title; that’s silly. Wendy Darling is our human connection to the ageless boy clad in skeleton leaves.<br /><br />Peter Pan is a riot. He is sadistic, selfish, cocky, and mischievous. He enjoys killing pirates after waking them, and he basically makes the Neverland the universe that it is. This is not because Peter is a god, but it is because he is gay, innocent, and heartless. Only those like him can make it to the Neverland.<br /><br />This book is a pure treasure, and I can only say that my feelings for this book cannot be completely written down. So here’s a few gifs to show my range of emotions in <em>Peter Pan</em>:<br /><br /><img class="escapedImg" alt="Image and video hosting by TinyPic" src="https://d.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1380324611ra/690735.gif" /><br /><br /><img class="escapedImg" alt="Image and video hosting by TinyPic" src="https://d.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1380324611ra/690736.gif" /><br /><br /><img class="escapedImg" alt="Image and video hosting by TinyPic" src="https://d.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1380324611ra/690737.gif" /><br /><br /><img class="escapedImg" alt="Image and video hosting by TinyPic" src="https://d.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1380324611ra/690738.gif" /><br /><br /><img class="escapedImg" alt="Image and video hosting by TinyPic" src="https://d.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1380324611ra/690739.gif" /><br /><br /><img class="escapedImg" alt="Image and video hosting by TinyPic" src="https://d.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1380324611ra/690740.gif" /><br /><br /><img class="escapedImg" alt="Image and video hosting by TinyPic" src="https://d.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1380324611ra/690741.gif" /><br /><br /><img class="escapedImg" alt="Image and video hosting by TinyPic" src="https://d.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1380324611ra/690742.gif" /><br /><br />“<em>There could not have been a lovelier sight; but there was none to see it except a little boy who was staring in at the window. He had ecstasies innumerable that other children can never know; but he was looking through the window at the one joy from which he must be for ever barred.</em>”<br /><br />This basically sums up Peter’s conflict, one that he can’t bring himself to resolve. And it’s okay, because as a boy who never grows up, he isn’t able to develop to a high enough level to overcome the loss of his own mother.<br /><br />Oh my, this book did many things to me.<br /><strong>And I can’t wait for them to do it again.</strong><br /><br />Peter Pan.<br /><br />Why haven’t you all read this yet?<br />And if you have, why aren’t you freaking out at the people who haven’t read this yet?<br />Read this book now.<br />Buy it.<br />Read it.<br />Tell everyone about the boy who can’t grow up.<br /><br />Ghirardelli, out.<br /><br /><img class="escapedImg" alt="Image and video hosting by TinyPic" src="https://d.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1380324611ra/690743.gif" /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="11616159118918038906">...more</a>
